Officers
Daughters of Israel
Mrs. Jacob Bloom
also take an active role in the Red Cross bandage rolling
group . . . One of the past presidents of the Center
auxiliary, Mrs. Ben Chepenik, has been appointed a
director on the board of the Tuberculosis Association . . .
A recent project, successfully completed by the Daughters
of Israel, was that of filling kits of supplies for the
Pvussian War Relief. — R. G.
President Mrs. Jacob Bloom
First Vice President Mrs. Charles Fink
Second Vice President Mrs. Jack Becker
Third Vice President Mrs. Philip Selber
Recording Secretary Mrs. Haskell Zimmerman
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. William Gordon
Financial Secretary Mrs. Joseph Krestul
Treasurer Mrs. Joseph Blattner
★
The Daughters of Israel functions as the Jewish Cen-
ter auxiliary and its chief purpose is to aid the institution.
Its special project is to maintain the Hebrew and the
Sunday Schools, providing every Jewish child with an
opportunity to receive a Jewish religious education. In
order to encourage the children and give them a special
incentive, the organization offers prizes each year for
outstanding achievement and arranges suitable observ-
anees of the Jewish holidays. As an extension of its in-
terest in youth, the Daughters of Israel sponsors a Girl
Scout troop and tries to interest the sons of Center mem-
bers in joining the Boy Scout troop.
The Daughters of Israel have taken an ever-increasing
interest in the general life of the community. They
have sought to become extremely active in civic work.
Recognizing this, the USO has given the women repre-
sentation on its advisory board. Mrs. I. Gartner is the
official representative. The Red Cross sewing group of
the Daughters of Israel has met all quotas; its members
Jewish Center Confirmation Class? 1944

Senior Hadassah
Mrs. Maurice Cherry
fourth War Loan drive they sold over $460,000 worth
of bonds. They also provided entertainment for Jewish
servicemen, took charge of a booth during the tuber-
culosis seal campaign and aided the cancer control
drive.
The chapter conducted a monthly educational group,
a monthly Oneg Shabbat gathering, and a monthly pre-
sentation by a committee chairman of some special
Hadassah activity . . . The Youth Aliyah phase of Senior
Hadassah’s program is a beneficiary of the 1944 cam-
paign of the Jacksonville Jewish Community Council.
Officers
President.. Mrs. Maurice Cherry
Vice President Mrs. Harry Katz
Vice President Mrs. Herman Klausner
Recording Secretary Mrs. Lou Feigenbaum
Financial Secretary Mrs. I. Edwards
Social Secretary Mrs. Saul Perlman
Treasurer Mrs. Mac Presser
★
Hadassah, the Women’s Zionist Organization of
America, was started in 1912 by Miss Henrietta Szold
to foster Zionist ideals in America and to maintain and
support various activities in Palestine which contribute
towards its upbuilding as the Jewish National Home. Or-
ganized on a chapter and a membership basis, Hadassah
functions in 500 communities.
The Jacksonville Senior Hadassah chapter sponsors
the following projects, which are part of the national
program: (1) Youth Aliyah—funds for the maintenance
of and training of refugee children in Palestine admin-
istered by the Jewish Agency; (2) Hadassah Medical
Organization—a system of curative and preventive medi-
cal services; (3) child welfare projects, including kinder-
gartens, school lunches, etc.: (4) Jewish National Fund
collections of a traditional character; (5) Palestine sup-
plies, including linens, food, drugs, etc. for institutions.
The Jacksonville group during the past year also
supported the war effort. Members of Hadassah partici-
pated in Red Cross work, acted as blood donors and
volunteered their services at the USO building. In the
Elmer D. Romedy

Page Sixty-One
===== PAGE 13 =====
Capt. Colin Kelly, pilot, and Sgt. Meyer Levin, bombardier, were
member of the crew that sank the first Jap battleship, the Haruna,
from the air. Kelly died in that exploit; Levin was killed later,
after a series of heroic acts. In the battlefields of the air, as on
the playing fields of peace, Catholics, Jews and Protestants alike
find UNITY the only way to VICTORY.
Our Debt to America
In this war, we Jews are engaged not only be-
cause of our hatred for the enemy which has risen
to annihilate our existence, but for the love of our
America and for everything for which she stands.
That is why I want you, when you are learning
to kill, to remember to love; in your hatred of the
enemy, love humaneness, and love your brother
soldier regardless of his race or religion.
Ever since I came to America I have been look-
ing for an opportunity to express my gratitude
and affection for what I see in America. It hasn’t
been my privilege to pay debt in physical service.
I believe that I have tried to make my contribu-
tion towards the American Ideal by trying to
bring a better understanding between Jew and
Christian through their common religious inherit-
anee.
I believe I serve America by serving humanity.
Still I regret that this opportunity to fight physi-
cally for America has been denied me. I consider
myself serving America through you and your
efforts. But you, my boy, are not alone in the
service, for your parents who love you are with
you. All your ancestors are with you, not only
those of old, from your Biblical forefathers down,
but those ancestors whom you have accepted with
this country—these new patriarchs, Washington,
Jefferson and Lincoln and the ancestors who en-
dured the suffering of Valley Forge and those
resting in the fields of Gettysburg and in the For-
est of Argonne. Be of good cheer and be happy
you serve America.
—From a letter written by Sholem Asch to
his son, Pvt. John J. Asch.

Jacksonville Zionist District
William Silverman
Hadassah the District sent Miss Eunice Davis and Miss
Raela Fink to a three-day youth institute in Atlanta.
Similarly, the two groups furnished scholarships for
Miss Gertrude Meyerhoff and Miss Heidy Mayer to
attend the Brandéis Camp in the Pocono Mountains.
During the 1943-44 year, the following officers plan-
ned the activities of the District: Harry Katz, president;
I. M. Lieberman and Joseph Strauss, vice presidents;
Morris Bar-am, secretary; Jack Becker, treasurer.—H. K.
Officers
President William Silverman
First Vice President I. M. Lieberman
Second Vice President Joseph Strauss
Secretary Andre Bialolenki
Treasurer Jack Becker
★
A large delegation attended the Southeastern Zion-
ist conference at Savannah, November, 1943. The
Jacksonville Zionist District was signally honored for
having gained the largest increase in membership dur-
ing 1942-43. Following the conference, the local dis-
trict entered upon an intensive program against the
White Paper. This work was carried on through the
Emergency Committee for Palestine. The committee
caused thousands of letters and cards, hundreds of tele-
grams, and many resolutions to be sent to Washington,
calling upon the President of the United States and his
Cabinet, to use their good offices toward the abrogation
of the White Paper. In February, members of the Ameri-
can Palestine Committee were guests of the Zionist Dis-
trict at a luncheon in the Roosevelt Hotel.
Much attention was given the youth work. In April,
1944, members of the Zionist District and Hadassah
were addressed by Dr. Shlomo Bardin, Director of the
American Zionist Youth Commission. Jointly with

The Task of Religion
By MORRIS D. MARGOLIS
Rabbi, Jacksonville Jewish Center
Rabbi M. D. Margolis
As these words are being written (in the middle of
June), the armies of the United Nations are in the open-
ing phase of the liberation of Europe. The fate of the
world is hanging in the balance. Mothers and fathers,
wives and sweethearts, are anxiously praying for the wel-
fare of loved ones who are in the thick of battle. I hope
and pray that by the time we are ready to celebrate Rosh-
Hashonah, the anxieties and fears which now grip our
hearts will have been lifted.
Much has been heard about the kind of world that
we should like to see at the end of the war. All that has
been said and written is but a reflection of the dreams
of Judaism concerning a world condition in which men
will convert instruments of destruction into tools for
building a peaceful human society. When the conflict is
over we must not permit men to forget their humility
before God and go back to the arrogance and mean am-
bitions which so often accompany the acquisition of
power.
This, to my mind, is the task of religion. We can
best serve this high purpose through our own synagogue
and our educational institutions. The sense of humility
before God must dominate also our personal and com-
munal relationships. When differences of opinion arise,
we must not let them deteriorate into a mean, petty strug-
gle for domination of one faction over another, but at
all times, be mindful of the principle of fair play enunci-
ated by Hillel: "Do not do unto others as you would
not have others do unto you.”
Our sages said: "Great is peace, for it is a vessel
that contains all blessings.” 1 pray that when you re-
ceive this message into your homes peace will be near,
that our souls will rejoice in the prospect of happy days
to come. May your loved ones, well in body and spirit,
be restored to your homes. May the New Year bring
מ healing to the wounds of our people.—Amen.
Page Thirty-Seven
Forward Steps
By HARRY GENDZ1ER
President, Jacksonville Jewish Center
Harry Gendzier
It is my pleasure to report to the Center membership
that despite manpower shortage and hardships caused
by the war, our institution has been able to carry on all
of its functions with a fine record of achievement.
The educational facilities, so dear to most of us, are
being improved steadily and during the school year He-
brew education and the Sunday school have been avail-
able to an increased number of children . . . Religious
services have been held regularly . . . One of the high-
lights of the year was the Passover Seder, at which
hundreds of servicemen were the guests of the Center . . .
Looking forward to a rapid expansion of the Jewish
population of the City, the Center Board of Directors
is now working on a plan for increasing the cemetery
plot . . . The auditorium of our building has been in con-
stant use throughout the year by Jewish organizations
and by the Red Cross unit ... A fine spirit of coopera-
tion with the Jewish Temple has been developed, which
all of us hope will continue to flourish so that we will
benefit mutually.
Probably the greatest step forward taken by the Cen-
ter was the decision of the Board of Directors to initiate
a "Y” program, whose facilities will not be confined to
the Center members alone, but will extend to all who
wish to take advantage of them. This far-reaching move
should result in a real Center, whose activities will do
much to enhance both the usefulness and the prestige
of the institution. An able, experienced executive di-
rector has been engaged for the year 1944-1945.
During my first term of office as president of the Cen-
ter it has been a pleasure to work with so many fine
people toward a common cause for the good of Israel.
May real peace come soon with Victory and God grant
each of us happy and abundant years!

even take steps to find out whether he has this
ugly disease, because the Wassermann reaction,
which is used for the purpose, is the discovery of a
Jew. A Nazi who has heart disease must not use
digitalis, the medical use of which was discovered
by the Jew, Ludwig Traube. If he has a tooth-
ache, he will not use cocaine, or he will be bene-
fiting by the work of a Jew, Carol Koller. Ty-
phoid must not be treated, or he will have to bene-
fit by the discoveries of the Jews Widal and Weil.
If he has diabetes he must not use insulin, because
its invention was made possible by the research
work of the Jew Minkowky.
If he has a headache he must shun pyramidon
antipyrin (Spiro and Eilege). Anti-Semites who
have convulsions must put up with them, for it
was a Jew, Oscar Liebreich, who thought of chloral
hydrate. The same with psychic ailments: Freud
is the father of psychoanalysis. Anti-Semitic doc-
tors must jettison all discoveries and improve-

burg; the dermatologists, Judassohn, Bruno Bloch,

A Little Message To You
Armour’s exclu-
sive Texturation
process makes
VEGE TOLE a
smoother, whiter,
creamier short-
ening, that actu-
ally makes good
baking ever so
much easier.
First of all, it
saves you time,
because it actual-
ly creams with
sugar or blends
with flour in half
the time with
half the effort. It
makes perfect
mixing possible
without excess
handling of your
dough or batter.
And this is why Textured VEGETOLE makes your results
surer and the texture of baked things better. We asked the

Junior Hadassah
Miss Eunice Davis
form of a cocktail party, a donor dinner, a beach party
and a buffet supper. Members aided the Jewish Cen-
ter during its Chanukah banquet, took part in USO-
JWB events, cooperated with the Jacksonville Jewish
Community Council, participated in Friday evening ser-
vices, contributed to the Blood Bank and had charge of
the Young Judea clubs . . . Especially significant has
been the educational program of the local chapter, which
has sought to foster an appreciation of Jewish life and its
rich contributions to civilization.
Officers
President Miss Eunice Davis
Vice President Miss Jeanette Marks
Vice President Miss Ruth Shorstein
Vice President Miss Bernice Carlton
Vice President Miss Raela Fink
Corresponding Secretary Miss Doris Feigenbaum
Recording Secretary Miss Victoria Levy
Treasurer Miss Esther Smith
Reporter ., Miss Gertrude Meyerhoff
★
Junior Hadassah, the Young Women’s Zionist Or-
ganization, has more than 250 chapters, which, in addi-
tion to a Zionist educational program among its mem-
bers, conduct fund-raising campaigns for Palestinian
projects. The most important of these are: the Mier
Shfeyah village, which in the last year housed a pro-
gram for 180 children, many of them brought to Pal-
estine through Youth Aliyah, and the Henrietta Szold
School of Nursing, supported jointly by the Junior and
the Senior Hadassah. The Junior Hadassah also collects
funds for Youth Aliyah and the Jewish National Fund.
The Jacksonville chapter participates in these projects.
During the past year it also sponsored a Yom Kippur
Dance, a swimming party, a membership drive in the

Page One Hundred Fifty-Eight
===== PAGE 32 =====
Jewish National Workers’ Alliance
Brandéis Branch No. 295
Charles Kroser
Palestine), sponsors a number of schools in this country
and takes part in all Zionist efforts.
The Brandéis Branch, which is named after the vet־
eran Zionist leader, already has held a number of cultural
evenings and looks forward with anticipation to its first
full year of activity this fall.—W.I.B.
Officers
President Charles Kroser
Vice President Jack Becker
Recording Secretary Albert Robins
Financial Secretary Joseph Cohen
★
The Brandéis Branch of the Jewish National Workers’
Alliance is perhaps the newest organization formed in
Jacksonville. In February, 1944 a member of the na-
tional executive committee of the JNWA (commonly
known as Farband) visited the local community and in-
spired a group of people to apply for a charter from the
national headquarters.
The Jewish National Workers’ Alliance is a fraternal

Young Men’s Hebrew Association
Leon Shapiro
portant part in the entertainment of the Jewish service־
men. On Rosh Hashonah, a buffet luncheon was ar-
ranged after services and on Yom Kippur over 300 men
were served a breakfast at the conclusion of the fast.
During Succoth, Chanukah, Purim, Passover and Sho-
vouth, refreshments were served and services held.
The Y also has utilized its facilities to the utmost for
civilian needs. The rooms of the Y have been used as
a meeting place by a variety of organizations, even in-
eluding the local rationing board. On a number of oc-
casions transients were housed in the dormitory as an
emergency measure. Some time ago an athletic pro-
gram was instituted at the Y under the direction of Mose
Lafkowitz. Regular gymnasium classes are held twice
a week. They are open to the general public without
charge . . . Under the supervision of Mrs. Abe Shapiro,
and Mrs. Sam Bryan, the Red Cross Surgical dressing
workroom at the Y. M. H. A. has more than tripled its
quota this year. These workrooms are open Monday
night from 7:00 until 10:00, and all day Tuesdays.

Because of the need for an extension headquarters to
supplement the JWB-USO facilities on Duval street, the
YMHA has concentrated upon war service activities. The
statistics reveal an interesting picture. During the past
year over 15,000 men and women of the armed forces
visited the Y. The open house held every Wednesday
night catered to 3,000 servicemen. Over 100 service-
men’s wives were aided with personal problems, includ-
ing employment and housing. Engagement parties and
weddings were arranged for 15 couples. 1500 break-
fasts were served to servicemen on Sunday mornings at
a nominal cost.

Jews in Farming
There are well over 100,000 Jewish persons who
earn a living from engaging in agriculture in the
United States. The Jewish Agricultural Society, es-
tablished almost half a century ago to encourage and
aid Jews to go into farming, estimates that ap-
proximately 1,500,000 acres are cultivated by Ameri-
can Jews.

Temple Ahavath Chesed
Review of the Year
By Rabbi I. L. Kaplan
koff, Miss Carol Coleman, Mr. Sam Bucholtz and Rabbi
Kaplan. Lt. Calig of the Army Air Base taught the Con-
firmation class the last month. Miss Ida Goodfarb served
as secretary.
The Rabbi in the Community
During the course of the past year the spiritual leader
of the Temple was called upon to render service in many
capacities, not only in the discharge of his congregational
duties but also to speak before various civic and religious
groups. He has been asked to serve on a number of com-
mittees of an educational and cultural purpose. Whether
it be in behalf of Jewish, Russian or Chinese relief, he
gave himself in carrying forward these most deserving
enterprises.
A partial list of his engagements include addresses de-
livered before several church bodies of the Methodist,
Congregational and Christian faiths, giving book reviews
and stressing the note of goodwill and human relations.
He has spoken to a few Christian youth groups on Broth-
erhood. He has accepted the invitation to deliver the in-
vocation before a number of state and local conferences
and assemblies.
The National Conference of Christian and Jews in-
vited him to be one of a trio of speakers — Catholic, Pro-
testant and Jewish — addressing a large body of soldiers
at the University of Florida. The local Roitnd Table re-
quested that he speak to the senior and junior high
schools on the same platform with a Catholic priest and a
Protestant clerygman. The major broadcasting stations
have called upon him on several occasions to participate
in a panel discussion on some educational and civic mat-
ters. Every other Saturday he is heard on the Chapel of
the Air program over station WPDQ. He has also spok-
en before several Negro groups and churches. In the
absence of any religious leader, the neighboring com-
munity of Brunswick, Georgia recently invited him to
deliver a sermon. In planning for D-Day station WJHP
invited him, together with a Catholic priest and Protest-
ant minister, to record a prayer which was broadcast on
the day of invasion.
For twenty-five years the Rabbi has joined with other
religious bodies in holding an inter-denominational
Thanksgiving Day service. The Inter-faith Council of
the USO honored him on several occasions by having
him serve as Vice President.
The following are the names of persons who have
become members of the Temple since September 1, 1943:
Charles I. Brenner, Raymond R. Brice, Mrs. Ruth Hersh
Fountain, David Gershon, Milton Goldman, M. M.
Haimowitz, Henry Hildebrandt, Milton L. Hollins, Sol
W. Katz, Claude Levin, Max Lipsey, Oze S. Marcus,
Mrs. Jennie Nathan, Mrs. Rebecca Pearlstein, M. J.
Reiser, Mrs. Montague Rosenberg, Theodore T. Sandler,
E. M. Schloss, H. D. Siegel, B. Simon, Mrs. J. Simon,
Ira Stein, B. B. Yoffee.
The Religious School
In the eyes of some people, well meaning, perhaps,
the weakness of our congregational life lies in our reli-
gious school. They feel it is the link that definitely
needs strengthening, for without an educated and well
informed Jewish youth, the spiritual foundation of Juda-
ism is imperiled. These same people maintain that our
boys and girls are indifferent to their religious heritage,
for which the school is in great measure responsible.
Moreover, they cannot understand why we have so few
children enrolled.
I suppose it would please their egos if they could
boast of a large school. But numbers are not always
rhe criterion of a good school. If the few who are en-
rolled are thereby able to receive greater personal at-
tention in the classroom and the teacher can meet the
child on more intimate terms, thus doing more toward
character building, we ought to be delighted that we are
privileged to render this type of service. There may be
a possibility of a larger registration but this only can be
achieved if those members of the Temple who know of
any child who does not go to religious school, make it
their business to interest the child and the parents. Our
school is open to everyone - - to members and non-
members alike.
When measured in terms of efficiency and interest,
our school is doing a satisfactory job. Our curriculum
follows the course of studies recommended by the De-
partment of Synagogue and School Extension of the
Union of American Hebrew Congregations, whose text-
books are being used. Our boys and girls over a per-
iod of years do become familiar with the major aspects of
Jewish history, both Biblical and post-Biblical. They
are made aware of the significant ethical contributions
the Jew has made in the past and of the part he is to
play in the world today. All festivals are stressed and
the need to observe them in our homes is emphasized.
Ceremony and custom have a definite place during the
course of instruction. By the time they are ready to be
confirmed they should have an adequate knowledge of
their religion and their people. The Confirmation exer-
cises attest most eloquently to this fact.
Our teachers, untrained, but willing and earnest, give
of their talents and gifts with singular devotion. If our
parents would only exercise the same devotion and mani-
fest the same zeal in supplementing the classroom studies
our children would reveal astonishing results. But if
the elders are indifferent and slothful and permit their
children to absent themselves from school for no import-
ant reason, the blame for any apathy or lukewarmness on
the part of the child should be placed just where it be-
longs.
The staff of teachers during the past year included the
following: Mrs. Sam Bucholtz, Miss Arlene Spindell,
Mrs. Israel Kaplan, Mrs. Ben David, Mrs. Abe Chard-

Ladies’ Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society
Mrs. Sam Witten
stantial amount in War Saving Bonds . . . The Ladies’
Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society has cooperated with the
USO in sponsoring dances and receptions for the men
and women in the armed forces . . . Funds during the
past year were raised by dues, individual contributions,
chain card parties and a bond raffle.
Officers
President Mrs. Sam Witten

For over twenty years the Ladies’ Hebrew Sheltering
Aid Society has served the needy, supplying food, fuel,
clothing, medical care and housing. Through its non-
interest loan fund it has enabled many families to over-
come financial difficulties and re-establish themselves.
Transients are given food and lodging. Each year, at
Passover, money is sent to various institutions. Food is
bought and prepared for inmates in prison.
Because of war conditions, the Society’s hope of build-
ing a Home for the Aged has been retarded. Under the
leadership of Mrs. Ben Yoffee members have continued
to raise funds for future use and have invested a sub-

this group led all other Jacksonville organizations in fur-

The Bnai׳ Brith Women of Jacksonville is part of a
national organization of 60,000, whose womanpower,
resources and energies are serving the nation’s wartime
needs with the same zeal with which they support proj-
ects for the advancement of education, youth welfare,
community service and Americanism. Although the
youngest women’s organization in Jacksonville, Bnai
Brith Women already has an enviable record of activi-
ties. Its principal local objectives are to assist A. B.
Weil Lodge and to aid Hillel, the AZA and the Bnai
Brith Girls.

Business and Professional Women’s Division of Hadassah
it played a vital part in obtaining home hospitality for
the service men and women stationed in this locality . . .
The closing of the doors of Palestine at a time when the
Jewish refugee problem is so great will render the work
of such Zionist groups as the B and P Women even more
far-reaching during the next few months.—NG.

The Business and Professional Women’s Division of
Hadassah closely follows the program established by the
Jacksonville Chapter of Senior Hadassah. It includes
that portion of the Senior group which is unable to at-
tend the monthly meetings because of business or pro-
fessional demands. The B and P Division during the
past year has aided in filling every quota in Senior Ha-
dassah projects: Hadassah Medical Organization, Child
Welfare, Jewish National Fund, Youth Aliyah, and Pal-
estine supplies.
The Business and Professional Women’s Division of
Hadassah has contributed time and energy to important
nationwide programs, the sale of War Bonds, the Amer-
ican Red Cross, and local USO needs. Particularly has

Page Fifty-Nine

This Is America!
Courtesy Appreciate America, Inc.
Dewey Speaks on Palestine
"As the success of our fighting forces bring us
closer to ultimate victory, the significance of Pal-
esdne becomes greater every month. We also come
closer to the necessity to remember well and hard
the lessons of the First World War. The peace
which followed World War I failed to solve the
problems confronting the minorities, and that fail-
ure contributed to bringing about the present cruel
conflict. The methods followed by Axis propa-
ganda in the Middle East showed clearly how the
problem of minorities and the dilemma of Pales-
tine became a strategic factor for the forces of
hate and oppression.
"It is essential that a way be found to open the
doors of Palestine, especially with so many doors
elsewhere which remain closed. This question has
ceased to be the concern of merely one or two na-
tions. It is the concern of men of good will all
over the world. There will be no easy solution.
At the same time, if no home is found for hundreds
of thousands of helpless and wandering Jews, the
problem will be magnified throughout the entire
world. That is a spectacle which no Jew could
tolerate and no Christian could observe with any-
thing but abhorrence.
"The doors of Palestine must be permanently
opened.” — Thomas E. Dewey, Governor of New
York.

Page One Hundred Thirty-Six
===== PAGE 46 =====
Smile of Achievement
This young refugee farmerette proudly holds the first fruits of
her labors as a tiller of the soil. Behind her is a wall of the van in
which her family’s worldly possessions were brought to Palestine
from Czecho-Slovakia before the war broke out. That van was
converted into their home in a Palestine settlement until funds
provided by American contributors to the United Jewish Appeal
made possible the construction of new living quarters.
The Racket of Anti-Semitism
by WILLIAM DRAPER LEWIS
Director, American Law Institute, Philadelphia
Can America be protected from the use of anti-
Semitism as a racket, as a means of helping Fase-
ism, as an instrument for destroying the spirit of
unity for the war effort and so prolonging the per-
iod for the destruction of lives? America is today
confronted with many vital questions but none
more vital than this. The evil results of the spirit
of race prejudice and hatred threaten our future
and our children’s future. It is the darkest cloud
on our national horizon today because it is a di-
rect denial of those principles, even our imper-
feet recognition of which has made possible the
strength of our nation and, what is more import-
ant, a way of life that gives us a high average
of happiness.
The consequences of anti-Semitism are clear;
clear also the lines along which it should be com-
bated. Little can be done by legislation. You
cannot legislate out of a man his hate and con-
tempt of another, or, except within narrow limits,
compel him to associate on terms of equality with
those he dislikes. To attempt by legislation to
suppress the public expression of a dislike for
Jews, except where such expression threatens pub-
lie disorder, sets a bad precedent: Freedom of ex-
pression is the cornerstone of individual liberty.
To be combatted, anti-Semitism must be attacked
at its root. The root is the denial of the sacred-
ness of personality, the denial of democratic ideals
based on the equality of man. It is the denial of
the basic teachings of Jesus. The Jews them-
selves can doubtless do much but it is principally
by non-Jews that this battle will be won or lost.

There’s Plenty of
ELECTRICITY
But there isn’t Any to Waste
Wasted electricity means waste of fuel, equipment and transpor-
tation . . . essentials of war that go into the production of elec-
tricity. Waste slows down our war effort, delays the day of
Victory. That is why it is common sense for all of us to join
with our fellow Americans in using that electric service we need
but being very sure we need what we use Waste is always use-
less extravagance, in time of war it is inexcusable.
CITY ELECTRIC DEPARTMENT
GEORGE A. PIERCE, Commissioner of Utilities
Utility Building. Foot of Laura St.

Basic English
is a subject which is being: much discussed these
days. Briefly, basic English is an ultra-simplified
version of our language, with a vocabulary of
only 850 words, which is being proposed as a sort
of universal language through which all the peoples
of the world could communicate with each other.
Reading about basic English the other day, it
occurred to us that since the day this Agency was
started, we have taken pride in the simple, non-
technical analyses we make of our clients insur-
anee needs. Similarly, knowing that insurance
terms are somewhat unfamiliar to the general pub-
lie, we have always endeavored to make sure that
each client clearly understood the exact scope and
meaning of his policy. Just one of those “extra
services’ that more and more people are learning
to associate with the Diamond Insurance Agency.
Give us a trial won’t you?
Diamond Insurance Agency
ALL FORMS OF INSURANCE
TELEPHONE 5-1123

Page Seventy-Two
===== PAGE 57 =====
Aleph Zadik Aleph
Abbey I. Fink
YMHA . . . Alephs in the service were kept informed
through correspondence about the chapter’s activities . . .
A paper drive netted over four tons of scrap paper, re-
ceiving recognition from the Duval County Salvage Com-
mittee ... A successful War Bond drive merited a certif-
icate from the Supreme Advisory Council of AZA . . .
Every other week members of the chapter gather in the
YMHA and roll bandages for our armed forces.
Officers
Aleph Godol (President) Abbey I. Fink
Aleph Sigan (Vice President) Sol Proctor
Aleph Mazkir (Secretary) Beryl Weinstein
Aleph Gisbor (Treasurer) Aaron Perlman
Advisor Roland Krantz
Advisor Harry I. Jaffe
Advisor M. J. Reiser
Athletic Advisor Melvin O. Margol
★
National AZA Sabbath was observed by the local
chapter in November. Beryl Weinstein gave the ser-
mon which received honorable mention in the National
AZA Sermon Contest. A Friday service was given by
the chapter in honor of Captain William Katz, alumnus
of the chapter, who was home on leave. Life member-
ship cards were presented to the mothers of the mem-
bers in service . . . The social highlight of the year was
the dance held in February, 1944, at which Miss Miriam
Portnoy was crowned "AZA Sweetheart.” In April
the chapter staged its seventh anniversary dinner at the
Seminole Hotel.
In line with the National War Service program of
AZA, the chapter sponsored several USO nights at the
Young Judea
There are two senior Young Judea clubs: the Henri-
etta Szold Club, under the guidance of Miss Raela Fink
and Miss Eunice Davis, and the Daughters of Judea, led
by Mrs. Sidney Stillman . . . The Daughters of Judea
originated and sponsored a successful bazaar, the pro-
ceeds going to the Keren Hanoar Fund. The Meyer
Levin and Henrietta Szold clubs each conducted a beau-
tiful Sabbath service at the Center. All clubs participated
in the Balfour Day celebration and in several Hadassah
programs.
The Yeung Judea movement strives to develop Jew-
ish boys and girls of adolescent age for Jewish leader-
ship and an active interest in Palestine . . . During the
past season two new Young Judean Clubs were organiz-
ed: the Meyer Levin Club, under the leadership of Miss
Marion Freed, is a junior club composed of 21 boys and
girls, ranging in age from 12 through 14 years;
the Theodor Herzl Club, originally sponsored by Mrs.
Nathan Newman, later by Mrs. Joseph Cohen, has the
10 to 12 year age group. Mrs. M. J. Reiser led the Rebec-
ca Gratz Club, the third junior group of boys and girls.

Page Thirty-Eight
===== PAGE 60 =====
The Human Problem
States own and control only their proportion of the na-
tional wealth. I knew firsthand that most Jewish
Americans were factory and white-collar workers ....
and I told him so. I said I knew Jews whom I disliked
very much as individuals just as I disliked some Catholics
and some Protestants, a certain Mormon, a number of
Slovenian and old-stock and Irish and Lithuanian Ameri-
cans. And I wound by by saying there was no Jewish
problem in America; there was no Negro problem, or
Czech, Polish, Irish, Armenian ... or German problem;
there was only the American problem — the human
problem, the same here as elsewhere.
An hour before my Czech-American friend had al-
most wept over Hitler’s seizure of Czechoslovakia. But
now, paying no attention to what I had said, he sug-
gested a plan for what "we, the non-Jews, ought to
do with the Jews; round them all up and exile them to
the Dakotas! Let them do what they like there, but no-
where else in this country."
"You mean a kind of concentration camp,” I said.
"Call it what you like," he said. "They get the best
of you."
"That’s exactly what Hitler is doing in Europe,” I said,
"and what he hopes will happen here. You’re helping
him."
— (Two-Way Passage, by Louis Adamic.)
One day I was walking on Michigan Boulevard in
Chicago with a Czech-immigrant businessman who is an
American citizen and in most respects a fine, intelligent
man. He was interested in my "Plymouth Rock and
Ellis Island" ideas and agreed with most of them.
"But — " he abruptly stopped in the shadow of the
Tribune Building—"what are you going to do about
the Jews?"
"What do you mean?” I said.
"Well, they have all the money."
"They haven’t either," I said, reaching into my pocket;
"Ive got a few dollars myself."
"I mean they have all the business."
"They haven’t either; you’re in business and, so far
as I know, you’re not on the verge of bankruptcy."
"I mean they control everything."
"What—for instance?"
"Everything!”
I said, "The Tribune here is one of the biggest things
in Chicago; do the Jews control it?”
"No, but —
"But what?"
"Nothing; you’re too damn precise.”
Some time before I had read an excellent article in
Fortune magazine showing that the Jews in the United

Girl Scouts of America
T roop 2
Mrs. Louis Slott Beverly Safer
Beverly Safer and Sabina Reiser were chosen to rep-
resent Troop 2 at the: city-wide week-end at Camp
Chowenwah, Girl Scout Camp at Green Cove Springs.
They brought back numerous ideas for games, songs and
out-of-door cooking . . . On January 14, Arbor Day, a
very interesting program was presented, during which a
dogwood tree was planted on the east side of the Cen-
ter . . . During the past year the troop secured the
services of two new leaders, Miss Rachel Rothstein and
Mrs. Sarabelle Holbert. The final meeting of the year
was in the form of an all-day hike and cookery event on
the shores of the St. John’s River.

Mrs. I. Gartner Mrs. Ben Chepenik

Girl Scouts of America, Troop 2, is sponsored by the
Daughters of Israel. Its membership includes Jewish
girls between the ages of 10 and 14 . . . Besides their
regular activities of camping, homemaking, arts and
crafts, etc., the girls last year sold over $30,000 worth
of U. S. War Bonds and stamps during the Fourth War
Loan Drive, receiving a citation from the Surgeon Gen-
eral of the U. S. Army for this achievement.
Boy Scouts of America
Troop 14
Ben Chepenik
Stein acted as leader of the Wolf Patrol, Herbert Sohn
was in charge of the Flaming Arrow Patrol and Edwin
Safer headed the Bat Patrol.
Off icers
Scoutmaster.. Ben Chepenik
Jr. Asst. Scoutmaster Mordy Rosenkranz
Senior Patrol Leader Sol Proctor
Scribe Herbert Sohn
Quartermaster and Librarian Robert Stein
★
Troop 14, Boy Scouts of America, is sponsored by a
committee of the Jewish Center. Under the leadership of
Mr. Ben Chepenik it has been forging ahead as one of
the most active groups of boys affiliated with this na-
tional youth movement. During the year the troop took
part in the usual hikes, campaign trips and other events
which normally constitute the scouting program.
In addition to the troop leaders listed above, Robert
Page Eighty-Three
===== PAGE 67 =====
The Facts Are ..
• At least one third of the Jewish
physicians of the nation are serving
with the armed forces.
• Five hundred and twelve Jewish
families in America have given a to-
tal of 1791 sons to the armed forces.
Fifteen of these families have 98 sons
in the service.
• The Distinguished Service Cross,
the second highest Army award for
heroism, has been given to 29 men of
Jewish faith.
• Thirty-four percent of the Jewish
male refugee population of the Unit-
ed States are bearing arms for their
adopted country.
Twenty-five percent of the Jewish
inductees from a typical industrial
city are serving in what is commonly
regarded as the most dangerous
branch of the service^the Army Air
Forces.
1 House-to-house canvasses in a num-
ber of representative cities show that
the percentage of Jewish men in serv-
ice is in excess of the ratio of Jews to
the general population.
1 Studies by The Bureau of War Rec-
ords indicate that more than 400,000
men of Jewish faith are serving in the
armed forces of the United States; or,
in military terms, more than 25 divi-
sions.
• More than 5,000 Jewish men in uni-
form have received citations for val-
or and merit, often posthumously.
• Among Jewish servicemen from
New York City alone over 1,100
casualties were already recorded and
many more remain to be authenticat-
ed.
• Fifty percent of all rabbis in Ameri-
ca have volunteered for military duty
as chaplains.
• Thirty-seven Jewish men have receiv-
ed ten or more awards of valor and
among them hold an aggregate of
405 such awards.

Music to His Ears
While alien - baiting Sen. Reynolds and Rep. Rankin rant against
“refugees,״ scores of Jewish refugee physicians like the one pic-
tured above are performing valuable service on the home front,
thereby proving that Hitler’s loss is America’s gain. The National
Refugee Service, to which Jacksonville residents contribute through
the annual Community Council campaign, has placed some 1500
doctors in various communities and has aided them in purchasing
the professional equipment with which to begin practice.

The Land Where Hate
Should Die
This is the land where hate should die׳-^
No feuds of faith, no spleen of race,
No dark brooding fear should try
Beneath our flag to find a place.
Lo! every people here has sent
Its sons to answer freedom’s call:
Their lifeblood is the strong cement
That builds and binds the nation’s wall.
This is the land where hate should die*׳־־
Thought dear to me my faith and shrine,
I serve my country well when I
Respect beliefs that are not mine.
He little loves his land who d cast
Upon his neighbor’s word a doubt,
Or cite the wrongs of ages past
From present right to bar him out.
This is the land where hate should die^
This is the land where strife should cease,
Where foul, suspicious fear should fly
Before our flag of light and peace.
Then let us purge from poisoned thought
That service to the State we give,
And so be worthy as we ought
Of this great Land in which we live!
Denis A. McCarthy.

Page One Hundred Eleven
===== PAGE 77 =====
Capital Myth
I have been in Washington the past month and
have had some opportunity to examine one of the
myths that have been anti-Semitically circulated
in the recent years: That the Government in
Washington is full of Jews . . . The story has been
of the same pattern as the one that Hitler gave to
the Germans in the years before he came to the
ill-fated position of fuehrer of the Third Reich.
His line was that the government of the German
Republic was largely Jewish.
My observation is that Jews are in the national
Government in rightful proportion to their number
in the population . . . When the anti-Semitic
mind speaks of "too many” Jews in the Govern-
ment, it is not giving out a democratic protest.
By "too many” Jews it means that even a few
Jews in the Government are too many. It wants
no Jews at all. Primarily it abhors the democratic
system by which men of whatever creed or race
may serve the Republic. Anti-Semitism is a
repudiation of democracy.
If I were to regard Jewishly the matter of Jews
being in the Government my pride would not be
in the fact that they are Jews but in that they are
serving the Government most creditably. I pre-
fer to look at Mr. Morgenthau as one American
looking at another and respecting him for magni-
ficent service to the nation; yet I can’t help looking
at him Jewishly as well and saying with consider-
able pride: "In the rectitude of his public service
this man fulfills the ethical imperative that goes
with being a Jew, and that’s the main end of being
Jewish.” A1 Segal.
Proud Jewish Fighter
A former college professor in Jerusalem, this soldier stands along־
side the truck which bears the official emblem of one of the Jewish
transport units in the British army. Soldiers recruited by the Jew-
ish Agency for Palestine have fought with distinction on every
battlefront from Syria to Italy.

Page One Hundred Fifty-Four
===== PAGE 78 =====
Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
1944 Beneficiaries (Continued)
NATIONAL JEWISH HOSPITAL—Treats people suffering from pulmonary tuberculosis. Non-sectarian.
NATIONAL JEWISH WELFARE BOARD—Conducts special activities for Jewish men in the armed forces; serves
YMHA’s and community centers.
RABBI ISAAC ELCHANAN THEOLOGICAL SEMINARY—Promotes study of Talmud; prepares students for ortho-
dox rabbinate and trains teachers.
YESHIVOTH—Six miscellaneous American yeshivoth, training students for the rabbinate along traditional orthodox
lines, derive funds from the Community Council campaign.
YIDDISH SCIENTIFIC INSTITUTE—Records, analyzes and publishes materials related to Jewish life and trains stu-
dents for Jewish research.
AMERICAN JEWISH CONFERENCE—Aims through joint consultation to arrive at decisions affecting problems of
rescue, post-war reconstruction and Palestine.
AMERICAN ASSOCIATION FOR JEWISH EDUCATION—Stimulates coordination of Jewish educational facilities.
AMERICAN ACADEMY FOR JEWISH RESEARCH—Fosters and promotes Jewish learning and research.
DROPSIE COLLEGE!—Offers graduate courses in higher Hebrew and cognate learning.
UNION OF AMERICAN HEBREW CONGREGATIONS AND HEBREW UNION COLLEGE—Former stimulates
Reform Judaism; latter trains rabbis for Reform pulpits.
Local and Regional Causes
ARMY-NAVY COMMITTEE (JWB)—Renders recreational, social and religious services to Jewish men in uniform
in the Jacksonville area.
FLORIDA STATE STUDENT UNION—Help to make possible Bnai Brith Hillel activities of Florida campuses.
HEBREW ORPHANS’ HOME—Located in Atlanta, this institution cares for dependent and destitute children of the
Southeastern area.
JEWISH EDUCATION—A fund has been earmarked to promote Jewish education in Jacksonville.
PUBLIC RELATIONS COMMITTEE—A specific activity of the Community Council, this committee works toward ef-
fecting better relations between the local Jewish and non-Jewish community.
TRANSIENT FUND—Gives financial assistance to religious itinerants passing through Jacksonville.
1944 Campaign Officers
Philip N. Coleman
Page Nineteen
President General Chairman
David H. Lasarow Philip N. Coleman

Page Sixty-Four
===== PAGE 89 =====
Jews and the War Effort
Texas who was the first to penetrate an important
enemy position. They fight, not as Jews or as Christians,
but as Americans.
A few weeks ago, when Mr. William C. FitzGibbon
presented to the Zionist Organization of America the
highest U. S. Treasury citation "for distinguished service
rendered on behalf of the War Finance Program,״ he
said: "If you had attained your objective, $25 million,
that would have been an outstanding success. But you
pressed on and on until your sales of War Bonds reach-
ed better than $65,000,000 . . . point to this with patriotic
pride, and challenge any other organization of like size
to top that achievement.״
Mr. Basil O’Connor, head of the Red Cross, speak-
ing of Jewish contributions in a totally different field—
Red Cross work—lauded "the widespread and valuable
participation of Jewish organizations and individuals in
Red Cross work,״ and asserted "that the American Red
Cross is fully aware of the debt which it owes these men
and women, both as individuals and groups.”
Yes, we Americans of Jewish descent are proud of our
patriotic services. We are confident that the wide masses
of American people will not fall prey to the insidious
propagandists who would foist Hitlerism on America
at the very time when our boys are giving their lives to
cleanse the world of the scourge of Nazism.
The full story of the extent of American Jewry’s par-
ticipation in the war effort, both at the home front and
in the fields of combat, is yet to be written. But when
such an objective evaluation is made, it will disclose that
Americans of Jewish descent had not only contributed
their due share of blood and sweat, of labor and money,
to the sacred cause for which we are fighting, but that
their gifts in labor, in services, in devotion and patriotism
have been excelled by no other group in American life.
We have been loathe to project Jewish accomplish-
ments and Jewish contributions to the war needs. But
for the fact that certain elements in this country who
have become infected with the leprous Nazi germ have
been disseminating vicious, willfully false, malicious and
criminal propaganda that the Jews have not been doing
their share in this war—but for this blasphemy, deli-
berately intended to undermine the war effort by creating
racial and religious tensions—there would be no need,
except from the point of view of historical records, for
Jews, or any other group, to proclaim its endeavors on
behalf of the war effort. Surely, our men in combat
never stop to think whether it was a Jewish boy from
Manhattan who blasted a Nazi tank, whether it was a
Catholic youth from Boston who brought down a Nazi
plane, or whether it was a Protestant ranchman from

Editor
It should be noted that although the advertising reve-
nue is being shared by two institutions, the Temple and
the Center, because of the intervention of the Council
the book this year represents not simply a joinder of two
separate books, but assumes the character of a commun-
ity enterprise—the first Jacksonville Jewish Community
Yearbook. The evil of a twofold solicitation has been
eliminated.
A striking innovation in this Yearbook is the elimina-
tion of the individual membership listings of each
organization and their compilation into one master list,
coded for organizational affiliation. Although this has
meant the expenditure of infinitely more time than
under the old arrangement, the Editor feels that the ef-
fort was well spent. A glance at the master list now
makes it possible to see quickly the extent to which vari-
ous people have affiliated themselves in communal en-
deavors. It also reveals considerable overlapping in
membership, which is inevitable in a community the size
of Jacksonville.
The Editor assumes no responsibility for either the
spellings of names or the accuracy of the organizational
summaries. An effort has been made to correct names
in which the misspelling was obvious to the Editor. How-
ever, it is entirely possible that unfamiliar names remain
misspelled in the master list. If so, it is due to the fact
that the membership list which the particular organiza-
tion submitted was not correct. Except for instances in
which material has been abbreviated in the interest of
conserving space, the summary of each organization’s
work stands as submitted by the proper officer of that
group.
The Editor acknowledges his appreciation of the work
done by other members of the Yearbook staff who made
possible its publication. On behalf of the participating
organizations, he also calls the attention of the readers
to the advertisers listed elsewhere, without whose coop-
eration the Yearbook could not have been a financial
mccess.—W. I. B.
Hyman Selber
Chairman
In every age each organized Jewish community has
preserved in some form or another the happenings, large
and small, which, taken together, give a composite pic-
ture of how its people lived. These archives, fragmen-
tary though they may be in many instances, provide rich
source material for the historian.
Jacksonville has had no central source such as the old
Kehillah through which to assemble data on its develop-
ment. This may well become one of the functions of
the Jewish Community Council, whose new publication,
the Commentator, over a period of years will reflect the
life of the community. Until now the two religious insti-
tutions, the Temple and the Center, each has kept a par-
tial record of local activities through the medium of its
annual yearbook.
These two separate books have served a useful pur-
pose. At the same time, there has been duplication of
time, effort and materials. Neither the Temple nor the
Center confined itself to the story of its own function-
ing but included writeups on other organizations in
Jacksonville, so that in many instances the identical ac-
count appeared twice. The issuance of two separate
books also has meant that the same business concerns
were approached two times for advertisements. From
the point of view of sound public relations this had un-
desirable aspects.
Under the stress of wartime conditions, the inadvisa-
bility of continuing the arrangement of two separate
books became more apparent this year. It has been dif-
ficult to secure the proper quality of paper stock, print-
ing costs have mounted greatly and manpower shortages
have made it a hardship to get printing done on time.
When, therefore, the Jacksonville Jewish Community
Council, out of its interest in communal coordination,
used its good offices to effect a merger of the books,
both the Center and the Temple accepted the offer of
the Council to lend the services of its executive director
for the necessary editorial work. A joint committee was
formed to iron out minor details.

Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
1944 Beneficiaries
Overseas Causes
AMERICAN FRIENDS OF HEBREW UNIVERSITY—Supports the Hebrew University in Jerusalem, a permanent home
for the traditions of science and learning among Jews.
AMERICAN FUND FOR PALESTINIAN INSTITUTIONS—Supports 52 cultural, educational and social service in-
stitutions which do not receive funds from any other American source.
AMERICAN ORT FEDERATION—Aids vocational retraining of Jews through educational and technical schools, agri-
cultural colonies, cooperatives, etc.
EZRAS TORAH—Grants assistance to orthodox rabbis and religious scholars in need.
EUROPEAN AND PALESTINIAN INSTITUTIONS—24 traditional charitable and educational institutions receive
allocations from an annual miscellaneous fund.
HEBREW SEIELTERING AND IMMIGRANT AID SOCIETY—Conducts immigration activities on a world-wide
scale in collaboration with numerous local committees.
JEWISH TELEGRAPHIC AGENCY—Collects and distributes news about Jews all over the world.
NATIONAL LABOR COMMITTEE FOR PALESTINE—Provides funds for cooperative industries and labor settle-
ments in Palestine.
UNITED JEWISH APPEAL—Comprises the following three agencies:
(1) JOINT DISTRIBUTION COMMITTEE—Major American Jewish agency for world-wide relief.
(2) NATIONAL REFUGEE SERVICE—Assists newcomers to this country.
(3) UNITED PALESTINE APPEAL—Central instrument for building the Jewish National Home in Palestine
through the Jewish National Fund and Palestine Foundation Fund.
HADASSAH YOUTH ALIYAH—Brings children to Palestine from countries of Jewish persecution, and places them
in agricultural and vocational schools.
AMERICAN JEWISH CONGRESS—Fights against anti-Semitism and economic discrimination; endeavors to safe-
guard the rights of Jews everywhere.
National Causes
BNAI BRITH WIDER SCOPE—Maintains Hillel Foundations on college and university campuses, Vocational Serv-
ice Bureau and AZA youth activities.
COUNCIL OF JEWISH FEDERATIONS AND WELFARE FUNDS—Coordinates activities of welfare funds and
offers services in research and planning.
HEBREW THEOLOGICAL COLLEGE OF CHICAGO—Trains religious leaders for orthodox synagogues and instruc-
tors for religious schools.
HISTADRUTH IVRITH—Promotes knowledge of Hebrew language and literature in the United States.
JEWISH LABOR COMMITTEE—Through labor and fraternal organizations fights to protect religious, political and
civil rights of Jews.
JEWISH CONCUMPTIVES’ RELIEF SOCIETY—Maintains non-sectarian sanatorium for free treatment and care of
tuberculous patients.
JEWISH CONSUMPTIVE AND EX-PATIENTS’ RELIEF ASSOCIATION—Treats tuberculous patients who come to
Southern California; non-sectarian.
JEWISH PUBLICATION SOCIETY—Promotes, prints and circulates books of general interest on Jewish subjects.
JEWISH TEACHERS’ SEMINARY—Trains men and women for Jewish teaching, research and community service.
JEWISH THEOLOGICAL SEMINARY—Prepares men for the conservative rabbinate and teachers for religious
schools.
JOINT DEFENSE APPEAI. OF AMERICAN JEWISH COMMITTEE AND ANTI-DEFAMATION LEAGUE—
Raises funds for combatting anti-Semitic activities.
LEO N. LEVI MEMORIAL HOSPITAL—Gives care and treatment to indigent persons suffering from rheumatism,
sciatica and related ailments; non-sectarian.
NATIONAL FARM SCHOOL—Prepares city boys for careers as practicing farmers.

Boy Scouts of America
Troop 12
Bork and Albert Haimowitz won the five year service
cup; Nathan Sandler was given the advancement medal;
Ronnie Cohn and Louis Joseph were awarded the first
year advancement cup and Robert Wolf, the efficiency
medal . . . Three members of Troop 12 have become
Eagle Scouts. They are Nathan Sandler, Robert Wolf
and Sam Witten.
Troop 12 enjoyed an active year. In February the
troop entered the Scout Council inspection and won sec-
ond place. Later the troop entered two patrols in the
Scout Camporee and won a "B” and "C” rating. Dur-
ing the annual Parents’ Night in May awards were pre-
sented to the outstanding scouts in the troop. Sam Wit-
ten received the efficiency cup; Alvin Leitman, Alfred

===== PAGE 110 =====
A. B. Weil Lodge No. 800
Bnai Brith
Sidney J. Brown
To mark the founding of Bnai Brith, A. B. Weil
Lodge had two outstanding meetings. The first, in
January, took the form of a joint service with the Jewish
Center and was held at the Temple. Rabbi Amram
Prero of the Hillel Foundation at Gainesville was the
principal speaker. In March, a second joint service was
arranged, this time at the Center and a large class of new
members was initiated by a degree team composed of
Abe Newman, Philip Selber, Nat Shorstein, Harry Katz
and M. J. Reiser. Julius Fisher, secretary of the Fifth
District, gave the main address to the class which was
dedicated to David Davis. A joint meeting with the
Bnai Brith Women also was held in June at the home
of Mr. and Mrs. Morris Witten.
Another achievement to the credit of the lodge was
the organization of Bnai Brith Girls, a junior order of
Bnai Brith Women, with a charter membership in Jack-
sonville of about 35.
At present the lodge, already the largest men’s group
in the city, is striving to bring its membership up to 500
as a result of a drive headed by Leon Springer.—S. J. B.
Officers
.... Sidney J. Brown
Leonard Grunthal
Harry Katz
Maurice Bartley
Herbert Talisman
Edgar Felson
Morris H. Witten
Harry Jaffe
M. J. Reiser
Max Oberdorfer
B. Berk
David Davis
Max Moss
Abe Newman
Nat Shorstein
I. Silverberg
President

Vice President
Vice President
Recording Secretary..

Assistant Monitor
Assistant Monitor
Trustee
Trustee
Trustee
Trustee
Trustee..
Trustee...
★
The one hundredth anniversary year of the establish-
ment of Bnai Brith finds the local lodge concentrating
upon a war service program. Although 20% of the
membership is now in service (June 15), activities have
continued without interruption, including donations to
the Blood Bank, a war bond drive headed by David Har-
ris and the raising by David Davis of a $500.00 quota for
the National War Service Fund. Also included within
the category of war work has been the sponsorship of
Saturday and Sunday affairs for the men in service. Held
at the Temple and the Center in cooperation with the
USO, these have received the aid of Bnai Brith Women
and especially of Mrs. Morris Witten who was in charge.
Under the chairmanship of Harry Jaffe the lodge’s
AZA committee has made unusual efforts to assist its
junior order, the Jacksonville AZA chapter, in every pos-
sible manner.

Organizational Affiliations
The alphabetical list on the following pages contains the names of all who hold membership in a Jack-
sonville Jewish organization or who have contributed to the 1944 campaign of the Jacksonville Jewish Com-
munity Council. ^
Each organization has been given a code number. By consulting the explanation of this code which appears
at the beginning and at the end of the list, the reader may identify the affiliations of all people listed. Names of
husbands and wives have been listed together. However, wherever a son or a daughter in the same house-
hold belongs to an organization, that fact has been indicated by a separate listing and code number.
To illustrate, let us take these names under the letter "K”:
Krestul, Mr. and Mrs. Joe — 1, 2, 4, 6, 10, 13, 15, 16, 21
Krestul, Nathan — 18
Looking at the code, we learn that this means Mr. Krestul is a member of A. B. Weil Lodge No. 800
of Bnai Brith, YMHA, Jacksonville Zionist District, and the Jacksonville Jewish Center. (The latter member-
ship automatically extends to the entire family.) He also has contributed to the 1944 campaign of the Jack-
sonville Jewish Community Council . . . Mrs. Krestul holds membership in Senior Hadassah, Daughters of
Israel, Ladies’ Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society and Bnai Brith Women of Jacksonville . . . Nathan, the Krestuls‘
son, whose name appears separately, is a member of Boy Scouts, Troop 14.

Temple Choir
*
The Temple choir during 1944 included Mrs. Francis M. Holt, contralto and director, Mrs. Robert Lee Hutchinson,
organist, Mrs. Sidney Grossman, soprano, Mr. Stewart Comer, bass and Mr. Ernest White, tenor.
For the past several months Mrs. Russel Carlson, soprano, and Mr. Marvin Maher, tenor, have assisted the choir.
Mr. Herbert Zacharias has substituted from time to time for Mrs. Hutchinson at the organ and Miss Marion Freed played
the organ on Sabbath mornings.

National Jewish Welfare Board
Army and Navy Committee
Ira Stein
Despite many handicaps, the Home Hospitality Com-
mittee has been placing 150 servicemen a month in pri-
vate homes . . . The Social Affairs Committee has con-
tinued to sponsor Saturday evening dances and Sunday
open house at the Center or Temple. Each Wednesday
evening the Military Maids entertain . . . Through the
JWB extension program at the YMHA, sleeping accomo-
dations are available on weekends . . . The JWB office
also renders assistance to wives of servicemen who find
themselves hard pressed for living quarters or employ-
ment. — I. S.
Officers
President Ira Stein
Vice President Mrs. Sam Weiss
Secretary Marvin Schulman
Treasurer David Davis
★
The JWB Army and Navy Committee is the recognized
civilian agency serving Jewish military personnel away
from camp. The religious elements in its program al-
ways have been of prime importance. During Rosh
Hashonah arrangements were made in local homes, for
more than 250 men; on Yom Kippur they attended ser-
vices at the Temple and the Center, after which they were
guests at a YMHA break-fast and at the traditional
Yom Kippur dance in the Center.
During Chanukah, Chaplain Shaukman officiated at
YMHA services . . . Over 400 attended the joint Con-
gregational Seder on the first night of Passover. A second
Seder was held in the Center the following evening; simi-
larly the Temple acted as hosts to 350 men in the May-
flower Hotel. At Camp Blanding 1500 men took part in a
huge Seder . . . Throughout the year invitations were ex-
tended to servicemen to attend Friday evening services . . .
During Shovuos the YMHA held open house, serving a
buffet supper of traditional dishes ... A large quantity
of Bibles, prayer books and other religious material
regularly have been distributed to the men.
Ich Winsche Alie Meine Idishe
Freunde A Glicklichen Und A
Frenlichen Nein Yahr
&
S. A. (Steve) F R E E L

Jacksonville Jewish Center
Nat Shorstein

William I. Boxerman
Benjamin Baker
Philip Selber
Haym Salomon - ־ Patriot
interrupting the awesome service to raise $400,000.00
from the congregants at the behest of General Wash-
ington.
It is estimated that Salomon, whose official title was
"Broker to the Office of Finance,” gave $200,000.00 of
his own funds to the cause of the American Revolution.
When he died in 1785, he left his wife and four little
children penniless. The poverty-stricken family was
never reimbursed by the Government, although Com-
mittees of the House and Senate investigated and sev-
eral times recommended repayment.
More than any other Jewish person in the colonies,
Haym Salomon, who came to Philadelphia from Lissa,
Poland, in 1772, gave of his energy and substance for the
cause of freedom in America. Arrested and imprisoned
as a sympathizer with the Revolution when New York
was captured by the British, Salomon first helped Ameri-
can and French prisoners to escape and then fled himself,
going to Philadelphia, the mecca of the patriots.
In Philadelphia he began his never-ending efforts eo
secure funds for the ragged armies of George Wash-
ington. They were no limits to his zeal. Even the holiest
day of the Jewish religion, Yom Kippur, found Salomon
Good Wishes for the New Year

Depths of Misunderstanding
By ISRAEL L. KAPLAN,
Rabbi, Temple Ahavalh Chesed
Rabbi I. L. Kaplan
It is difficult to follow the depths of misunderstand-
ing that prevails among so many people whose knowl-
edge of the Jew is superficial and erroneous. Where
these men and women receive their information is dif-
ficult to trace. All we know is that they form certain
judgments which soon become stereotyped and part of
the mental furniture housed in petty minds. We all are
familiar with the stupid remark—"rich as a Jew.” You
and I know how absurd and fantastic this is. Yet how
widespread that belief is was brought home to me re-
cently when my telephone rang and a woman started a
conversation something like this:
"Is this the Jewish Temple and are you the Rabbi?
I am interested in buying a house and I would like to
borrow some money on a first mortgage. Do you think
you can secure the money for me?” I politely informed
the party that this was a House of Worship, not a mort-
gage brokerage institution. ”Why,” she said, "I know
that the Jews have plenty of money, so I thought I would
ask you as the head of the Jews here to help me get it.”
I tried to disillusion her about the so-called "riches”
of the Jew, about international Jewish bankers, and the
concentration of wealth in the hands of Jewish million-
aires. I hope I succeeded; yet I am not at all sure. The
belief that all Jews are rich has become part of the men-
tal make-up of so many people that it will require a new
technique to rid them of this notioti.
Page Twenty-One
A Grave Responsibility
By JOSEPH M. GLICKSTEIN
President, Temple Ahavath Chesed
Joseph M. Glickstein
Now that the liberation of Fortress Europa has been ac-
tivated by accomplished invasion, we know that final
victory is in process of being attained, depending upon
unity on our fighting and home fronts. The necessity
for such unity and coordination, among ourselves and
with our Allies, and the resultant strength in action there-
from, is not only obvious but statements emphasizing
such need are accepted as truisms without debate.
Is it not possible that members of our own faith are
overlooking an opportunity to learn by this world-wide
example? Is it not but stating simple truths to say that
intra-faith prejudices and evidences of intolerance must
be eliminated; that those fierce and furious religious an-
tagonists who feed the flame of controversy in Israel
must be tolerantly ignored; that our ancient faith should
not be used as a sounding board for a debating society
consisting of self-appointed leaders who do not lead;
and, that in our religious life, the line of demarcation
which properly separates purely religious matters as dis-
tinguished from purely political questions should always
be recognized?
We are all confronted with the grave responsibility
and solemn duty to see to it that in our own community
such unity shall exist and shall continue to exist and to
lend our efforts and influence in achieving the same
objective elsewhere.
Our people must present a united front or suffer the
consequences. A prime result of such unity, despite oc-
casional irritations in social life and in business and pro-
fessional spheres, and despite attacks from the lunatic
fringe, is bound to be real Victory.

Walter Levy
audiences, by specialists in the field of international
affairs, including: Prof. William Yandel Elliott, Dr. Wil-

★
A survey of the 1943-44 activities of the Brotherhood
reveals a year highlighted by several important episodes.
The monthly meetings consisted mainly of speakers who
brought to the members a message of interest. Among
the guest speakers were Chaplain Jacob K. Shankman of
the Naval Air Station, L. M. Rhodes of the State Mar-
keting Bureau and Dr. Lewis J. Velte of the USO.
The joint evening session with the Temple Sisterhood
proved to be a noteworthy event. Another project spon-
sored by the members included the Passover Seder at the
Mayflower Hotel for those in the armed forces, with 350
in attendance. The annual observance of the Temple
Brotherhood Sabbath was marked by an increased attend-
anee on Friday evening, May 5th. Lt. Col. Chaplain
Edwin V. Carter, Senior Chaplain at Camp Blanding,
delivered the sermon on "Brotherhood in Action.”
The Jacksonville Open Forum, which received the en-
dorsement and financial support of the Brotherhood,
served the community at large. Seven lectures were pre-
sented during the early part of 1944 before large Forum
Temple Confirmation Class, 1944

Elaine Grunthal and Alynne Edith Kunsberg.

Honor Roll
We are happy to present the following list of Jewish men from Jacksonville who have served or are now serv-
ing their country in World War 2. Although great pains have taken in the compilation of names over a period of
months, we are quite certain that this list is not complete. If there are omissions or inaccuracies, readers will
be rendering a distinct service to the Community if they will call these to the attention of the Jacksonville Jew-
ish Community Council, 7F Atlantic Bank Annex, phone 50060־. These names will form the basis for a communi-
ty־wide survey to be conducted this fall, in which full data will be sought, so that the participation of our local
men and women in the armed forces will be recorded in permanent form.
It would appear that already the percentage of those of Jewish faith from Jacksonville who serve Uncle Sam
is nearly nine per cent of the total Jewish population of Jacksonville. Since Jews constitute only two percent of the
total population of the City of Jacksonville, obviously our contribution to the armed forces, on a proportionate basis,
is very heavy. In the country at large, the overall percentage of the national population now in uniform is only
72/ו per cent.
From time to time in Jacksonville, as in other parts of the country, Nazi-inspired voices of hate have whispered
the lie that Jews are shirking their war responsibilities. Several times there has been distributed “the first Amen-
can״ propaganda, which slanders our honored dead. These calumnies make rolls of honor like the following even
more important. They represent irrefutable evidence that in the world-wide struggle against a Nazi tyranny that
has debased every principle of social justice for which Judaism traditionally stands, those of the Jewish faith, in
common with all other Americans, are ever ready to give the last full measure of devotion. The next time that
someone challenges Jewish patriotism when you're around, let him have these local facts. W. I. B.
Cooper, Chas. W.

===== PAGE 136 =====
Jewish Yearbook
Jacksonville
A Community Enterprise
JL me r ¿can Strength
By H. I. PHILLIPS
(Three American airmen, Edward Vogel, Izzie Goldberg
and Edwin J. Sipowski, killed in a take-off in San Juan, were
buried side by side with a Protestant chaplain, a Catholic
priest and a rabbi officiating. The flag for which they fought
flew over them.”—News Item.)
A chaplain, a priest and a rabbi—
Protestant—Catholic—J ew—
Three Yanks in three simple caskets—
Three colors, red, white and blue. . . .
A hush on a tropic island
As notes from a bugle fall—
Three rituals slowly chanted—
Three faiths in a common call!
A lad from the Bronx; another
Who joined up in Tennessee;
A third one from Waukegan—
A typical bunch, those three!
A crash in a naval airplane. . . .
A rush to its crumpled side. . . .
And nearby Old Glory marking
The reason the trio died.
They answerd a call to duty
From church and from synagogue—
From hillside and teeming city . . .
Three names in a naval log!
Each raised in his separate concepts—
Each having his form to pray—
But all for a faith triumphant
When rituals fade away!
A prayer in Latin phrases—
And one with more ancient lore;
A Protestant simple service—
All one on a distant shore!
“Qui tollis peccata mundi״ . . .
And, “Enter ye unto rest״ . . .
A blessing from ancient Moses . . .
For three who had met the test!
This is the story mighty
Making our sinews strong:
Boys from the many altars
Warring on one great wrong!
This is the nation’s power,
This is its suit of mail:
Land where each narrow bigot
Knows that he can’t prevail.
(Reprinted from The New York Sun)

Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
Second Annual Lyceum Series
MAURICE SAMUEL *-׳ Monday Evening, November 6 “The World of Sholom Aleichem”
Author, traveller and publicist, Mr. Samuel has been the interpreter of Jewish values for the past twenty-
five years.
FRIEDMAN and MICHAEL ׳—׳ Monday evening, December 4 Cavalcade of Jewish Music
A pianist - narrator and a baritone combine their talents. These artists scored a hit last year and are re-
turning, at popular request, with an entirely new repertoire.
DR. SALO BARON ־־׳* Monday evening, January 22 Jewish Community in Modern Times
Author and lecturer, Dr. Baron holds the chair of Jewish history at Columbia University. He is an emi-
nent authority on the above topic.
ROGER BALDWIN ׳—׳ Monday evening, February 12 Race Prejudice and World Democracy
Director of the American Civil Liberties Union, Mr. Baldwin has been the champion of the underprivileged
and of oppressed minorities.
IRVING DAVIDSON <—> Sunday evening, March 11 Jewish Wit and Humor
Wit and raconteur, Mr. Davidson is known as the Jewish Ripley, for his collection of historical oddities.
He is returning here at popular request this season, with a new stock of humorous stories.
Season Ticket for Entire Series of Five Events — $2.50
(including tax)
TICKETS OBTAINABLE AT 7F ATLANTIC BANK ANNEX, 5-0060
Place of each event will be announced later.

Jewish Welfare Society

! reasurer Joseph M. Glickstein

The past year marked a quarter century of service by
the Jewish Welfare Society to the Jewish community .
Although the war has altered somewhat the activities of
social agencies, certain fundamental problems still re-
main. Juvenile delinquency has increased and countless
problems arise because of the enforced separation of fam-
dies ... In war as in peace, there also are the aged and
the ill, the unemployable and the family deprived of its
breadwinner, all of which are the eternal problems of
the welfare organization . . . The Jewish Welfare Society
is a member of the ■Community War Chest.

The Pushaloter Ladies’ Aid Society was formed over
30 years ago to aid worthy causes in Pushalot, Lithuania.
All members of the society either were born in Pushalot
or are married to natives of that city. Until the Nazi in-
vasion severed contacts with the Jewish communities
abroad, money collected from the local members was sent
to Pushalot at regular intervals, for such causes as Pass-

Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
Executive Committee
icers
Ofh

William I. Boxerman
taken with skepticism by some and with a feeling that
it would entail a financial deficit. The response of the
public was so great, however, that over 650 season tick-
ets were sold and the series more than paid for itself.
As a result, an enlarged program of this' type is being
planned for the 194445־ season.
The Council renders many services of an intangible
character which cannot be described here; but there is
one concrete area in which the Council will function to
an even greater degree during the coming year—that of
public relations. Recently the executive committee ap-
proved a plan to enlarge the staff of the Council and
create a department of Community Relations. Headed
by a trained director, this Department will devote its time
and energy to the task of promoting inter-faith relations
and developing activities in the general community that
tend to strengthen the American way of life. In addi-
tion, it will deal with important internal problems. This
represents a far-reaching step in the Council’s develop-
ment as the responsible voice of the Jewish Community.
The Council has been extremely fortunate in the type
of leadership which it has attracted. When earlier this
year, David H. Lasarow, who had rendered four years of
yeoman’s service to the organization, asked to be given a
sabbatical leave from primary responsibility for direction
of the Council, the choice of the community at the an-
nual meeting of the Council fell unanimously upon Da-
vid Harris. Under his promising leadership the Council
looks forward to a period of ever-increasing usefulness in
the life of the community.—WIB.
During 1944 the Jacksonville Jewish Community
Council continued the process of growth which first was
intensified in January 1943 with the establishment of a
full-time office.Judged in terms of the number of work-
ers involved, number of contributors and total amount
raised, the 1944 campaign set an all-time mark, raising
41% more than the previous year. The size of the aver-
age contribution was about $159, quite a far cry from
the per capita gift in the first united drive in 1936, which
was approximately $19•
Perhaps even more important in some respects than
the financial success enjoyed by the Council is the in-
creasing prestige and influence which it is assuming in
the life of the community. More and more people are
looking to this organization as the planning body on
those problems which go beyond the purview of individ-
ual groups and affect the communal weal as a whole.
For over a yea! now the Council has been publishing
the Commentator, a bi-weekly which reaches the head
of every Jewish family. This newspaper has become a
permanent service in the community, not only as a means
of telling the Council’s own story, but as a medium for
such timely news on the Jewish scene as will stimulate
a greater interest in Jewish problems.
Noteworthy, too, has been the phenomenal success of
the first community-wide cultural series in the history of
Jacksonville. Beginning in November 1943 and once
a month for a period of 5 months, the Council presented
a cultural number. Although the need for this facility
had been evident for a long time, the project was under¬
