New Executive Director
By DR. HARRY O. H. LEVINE
Dr. Harry O. H. Levine, newly arrived director of the Cen-
ter, came here from Bangor, Maine, where he served as di-
rector of the Community Center, Executive Secretary of the
Welfare Federation and head of the Talmud Torah. Dr. Le-
vine will be in charge of the Center and “Y” programs. His
duties will include supervision of the youth program, ad-
ministration of staff and finance and inauguration of a com-
píete adult Center program. As an integral part of the
youth work he will direct the daily Hebrew School, Sun-
day School and the Nursery and Kindergarten departments.
Commenting on the significance of the appointment of the
director, Mr. Gendzier, president of the Center said, “This
marks another step forward in the Center program. Our aim
will be the development of an organization that will em-
brace the community in its scope. We aim to offer a positive
Jewish training for today’s youth and inaugurate a program
that will be meaningful to all ages.”
Dr. Levine received his B. S. in Sociology and Education at
the Massachusetts State College and his Ph D. at Dropsie
College. In the latter institution he majored in Semitics.
His thesis on “Halacha in Josephus” is the first comprehen-
sive study of all phase of Halacha of that period. Dr. Levine
also did graduate work at the Silver Bay New York School
of Social Work and attended various other institutions deal-
ing with specific phases of group work. At Jerusalem he
matriculated at the Hebrew University. Soon thereafter,
the 1936 riots broke out and Mr. Levine joined the British
forces and was placed in charge of the Jewish colony of
Kiryat Anovim, where he served to the end of the disturb-
anees.
Mr. Levine took an active part in community affairs. He
served as Treasurer of the Council of Social Agencies, was
Chairman of the Recreation Committee which inaugurated a
city wide summer program which served over 4000 children
during 8 summer weeks. He was a member of the City of
Bangor Recreation Commission, Chairman of the Public Re-
lations Committee, member of the USO Council, USO Staff
and an advisor to the Columbia Street USO Unit. Dr. Levine
was Jewish Welfare Board representative at Dow Field and
flew on a special mission for the USO Overseas to Newfound-
land, and was also civilian chaplain for the United States
War Department. Dr. Levine was Hillel Counsellor at the
University of Maine and Colby College and was in charge of
programming and religious organization at the N.Y.A. Unit
at Queddy.
“Fundamentally the Jewish Center is an educational in-
stitution,” said Mr. Gendzier. “It places its reliance upon the
solid forms of growth through knowledge, understanding,
dramatic performances, supervised dancing, literary clubs,
social groups, scout work and tournaments.”
Mr. Gendzier explained that Jewish Centers, similar to the
Jacksonville institution, are intended to create a Jewish social
life as an integral part of the social life of the community as
a whole, recognizing, however, that Jews, like non-Jews have
social needs and desires and that some of these needs and
desires can be met only by Jews themselves. “Those Cen-
ters ” Mr. Gendzier concluded, “add to the enrichment of
the civic life of American cities.”
for Bar Mitzvah. He thus has endeared himself to many
scores of students whom he has inspired with a reverence
for religious observance. In addition he has a multi-
plicity of other functions relating to the daily conduct of
the Center, including supervision of the daily minyon,
reciting memorial prayers, collection of dues, etc. His
kindly personality has been a great influence in creating
good will for the Center in many quarters.
The Sunday School provides a ten-year course in his-
tory, customs and ceremonies . . . Total registration was
about 235, with the school in charge of Dr. Bar-Am, for
part of the year, and Rabbi Margolis, for the balance of
the term ... In December the Sunday School presented a
Chanukah playlet and in March, a Purim operetta based
on Hebrew and Yiddish folk songs ... In April the
Daughters of Israel religious committee sponsored a
demonstration Seder by the children . . . The school
closed in May with the presentation of prizes and awards
. . . Confirmands and graduates were honored at a sup-
per party given by the Daughters of Israel, which resulted
in the formation of a Jewish Center Alumni Association
. . . During the course of the year Sunday School pupils
contributed $300 to the Social Service fund, benefiting a
number of worthy agencies ... 50 trees were planted in
the JNF forest, honoring Abe Newman, past president
of the Center.
The teachers’ staff included Miss Ruth Leonard, Mrs.
M. J. Reiser, Miss Leah Setzer, Miss Sylvia Haber, Miss
Victoria Levy, Mrs. Philip Selber, Mrs. Nat Cohen, Mrs.
Charles Kluglose, Miss Raela Fink, Miss Mollye Mizrahi
and Messrs. Morris B. Wilson, M. J. Reiser and Andre
Bialolenki.
Cemetery Committee
The Cemetery Committee of the Center (Chevra Ka-
disha) continued, as in previous years, to supervise the
Center Cemetery, which was repurchased seven years ago
from the Evergreen Cemetery Association. Max Rubin
is chairman and Oscar Magezis acts as overseer of the
grounds. Although an adjunct of the Center, the com-
mittee, consisting of ten members elected by the Center
board, handles its own finances. An auxiliary commit-
tee, the Shamoshim committee headed by Rev. Robin,
assists the Chevra Kadisha in performing all burials ac-
cording to orthodox ritual. .. Because there are now very
few burial plots available in the present cemetery, nego-
tiations are being conducted for the purchase of an addi-
tional burial tract.
Members of the Cemetery Committee are: Max Rubin,
chairman; Benjamin Baker, Louis Bucholtz, Joseph
Hackel, I. M. Lieberman, Oscar Magazis, Max Mirkis,
David Moscovitz, and Max Rose.
Standing Committees
Standing Committees of the Center for the year 1944
are as follows:
Religious: Jack Becker, chairman, B. Baker, Oscar
Magezis, Joseph Hackel and David Moscovitz; Boy
Scout: Ben Chepenik, chairman, Joe Strauss, Joe Becker
and Max Rose; Cultural: Philip Selber, chairman, Philip
Bork, William I. Boxerman, Robert M. Rieders and Abe
Newman; House: Hyman Selber, chairman, Max Rubin,
M. Wolf son, S. D. Kramer and Oscar Magezis.
Education: Joseph Hackel, chairman, Max Mirkis, D.
H. Lasarow and Philip Selber; Membership, Joe Bartley,
chairman, Ira Stein, Abe Selber and I. M. Lieberman;

Neiv Year’s Greetings From Abroad
Jewish men and women are serving with the armed forces and American Red Cross in every theatre of
war. Several in Italy, representative of their brothers and sisters everywhere, asked the Red Cross to convey
greetings to their people back home.
SGT. EDWARD DUBINSKY
St. Louis, Mo.
“Our prayer this year as we
gather in the field and at the
for enlarging public participation
hence we may pray with our lov-
ed ones at the synagogue at home.
With the world weary and work־
ing for the restoration of the hu-
man dignity, we in the field ap-
predate the efforts and countless
services of the American Red
Cross.”
New York City
“Though we may be hoping for
a real peace soon, each of us must
keep plugging away at our jobs.
Only in that manner will we as-
sure the earliest possible return
to peace.”
CPL. HERBERT R. LOTZ
Bronx, N. Y.
“My greetings for the coming
year also include the fervent wish
for enlarging publis participation
in American democracy. Only in
this manner will substance be giv-
en to the bitter and bloody bat-
ties of today.”

To Cheer Their Homecoming
Millions of Russian families, returning to their homes in the wake
of Red Army victories, lack the most elementary necessities of life.
Above, 1. to r., are Mrs. I. Gartner, Mrs. Charles Fink and Mrs. Ben
Chepenik, as they pack part of the 100 kits being filled by the
Daughters of Israel to help cheer the homecoming of these Rus-
sian civilians. Other members of the committee are Mrs. M. D.
Margolis and Mrs. Joseph Krestul (who together with Mrs. Chepe-
nik are co-chairmen of the undertaking), Mrs. Jacob Bloom and
Mrs. Jack Gartner.
Each kit, which is being prepared in cooperation with Russian
War Relief, Inc., is of standard size and contains an assortment of
specified commodities, including sugar, evaporated milk, dehydrated
soup mix, cigarettes, etc., as well as a personal message of sympathy
and good will from the sender.

George Washington and the Jews
by the indulgence of one class of people that another en-
joyed the exercise of their inherent natural rights, for
happily the Government of the United Nations, which
gives to bigotry no sanction, to persecution no assistance,
requires only that they who live under its protection
should demean themselves as good citizens.”
For Jews whose current philosophy of Americanism
and internationalism is not always based on knowledge
of history, it is pertinent to remember the sanguine words
which Washington addressed later to the Savannah Jews.
"I rejoice,” he wrote way back in 1790, "that a spirit
of liberality and philanthropy is much more prevalent
than it formerly was among the enlightened nations of
the earth, and that your brethren will benefit thereby in
proportion as it shall become still more extensive; happily
the people of the United States have in many instances
exhibited examples worthy of imitation, the salutary
influence of which will doubtless extend much fur:
ther ...”
Perhaps when next they pay tribute to the Father of
our Country, Jews will also notice that George Wash-
ington saw no ill in speaking of Jews who did not enjoy
the blessings of living in this land as our "brethren.”
All Americans rejoice yearly in remembrance of the
spirit of the "father of his country” on the occasion of
the birthday of George Washington. For Jewish citizens
there is special stimulation, as well as poignancy, in re-
calling the several letters which President Washington
sent to a number of Jewish congregations which felicitat-
ed him on the work of his administration. His letters
are masterpieces in their summary of the American way
of life in which Jews, with all others, would have the
״ight to share fully.
Moses Seixas, writing in Hebrew to Washington on
behalf of the Jews of Newport in 1790, spoke of the
"deep sense of gratitude to the Almighty Disposer of all
events” for "a government erected by the majesty of
the people, a government which gives no sanction to
bigotry and no assistance to persecution, but generously
affording to all liberty of conscience and immunities of
citizenship, deeming every one, of whatever nation, ton-
gue or language, equal parts of the great governmental
machine.”
In his classic reply to the Newport congregation, Wash-
ington enunciated a doctrine which is still a fine ob-
jective (it not having been fully attained as yet) : "It
is now no more that toleration is spoken of as if it were

Jacksonville Jewish Center
Review of the Year
Social and Cultural Events
An outstanding event was the Chanukah banquet, at
which the Center mortgage was burned. Dr. Max Arzt
of the Jewish Theological Seminary gave the principal
speech. Mrs. Harry Finkelstein and Mr. Morris Wolf-
son were given the honor of actually burning the mort-
gage ... A Purim concert held in the auditorium on
March 12 featured a group of artists from the armed
forces, who rendered a rich program of classical and pop-
ular music. The Jewish Chapel Choir of the Naval Air
Station also contributed several numbers which were ap-
plauded warmly.
In addition to filling several out-oTtown engagements,
Rabbi Margolis also stimulated several local Zionist
groups through his review of "The Forgotten Ally,” and
spoke on Zionism several times at meetings of the Jack-
sonville Zionist District.
Educational System
The daily kindergarten, with an enrollment of 40 chil-
dren, was under the direction of Mrs. M. J. Reiser, as-
sisted by Mrs. R. S. Lourie and later, by Mrs. Charles
Kluglose. In addition to the ordinary kindergarten fea-
tures, this school trains children in the observance of
Jewish customs and ceremonies, teaches them simple He-
brew words and utilizes their handicraft for the creation \
of Jewish religious objects. Through these pleasant
associations the youngsters become attached to the Center
and are prepared for more advanced study.
Rev. Joseph Robin Rev. B. Safer
The Hebrew School serves the entire community, irre-
spective of Center affiliation. Its curriculum aims to
synthesize Jewish culture with the highest concepts of
Americanism. Modern textbooks and pedagogy are em-
ployed to achieve this end. More than half of the 89
children registered for the year were between the ages
of seven and nine. The total enrollment was distributed
among seven grades in accordance with age, ability and
prior knowledge . . . Seven graduates of the Talmud To-
rah received their diplomas at the April exercises ... In
May, 22 third grade children participated in a Chumosh
party, in which they demonstrated their knowledge of the
first chapter of the Book of Genesis.
The staff included Rabbi M. D. Margolis, principal,
Dr. Morris Bar-Am, Mrs. Harry Abrams, Mrs. Carl Al-
pert and Rev. Joseph Robin. Rev. Robin this fall begins
his fourteenth year of service with the Center. In the
Hebrew School he acts as a teacher, preparing the boys
Page Forty-Three
The Religious Cycle
The year opened with the High Holiday services.
Rabbi Margolis officiated in the Center Synagogue and
Rev. B. Safer, in the auditorium. Both places of wor-
ship were filled to capacity. Servicemen were accomo-
dated in the synagogue. Home hospitality was provided
by Center members . . . On October 3rd the annual me-
morial services were held at the Center Cemetery . . . On
October 4th two plaques were dedicated, containing the
names of over 200 Jewish men in the armed forces.
Chaplain Paul W. Ludden was guest speaker . . . The
Suecoth holiday ended with a Simchath Torah celebration
on October 21st.
Despite war conditions, the Friday night services be-
ginning October 29 were fairly well attended, as were
also the following special Sabbath events: a Balfour Day
observance, November 5, in which the Zionist groups
cooperated; AZA Sabbath, November 12 and March 24;
Thanksgiving service, November 26; joint service at the
Temple, with Rabbi Prero as guest speaker, January 28;
installation of officers, February 4; Brotherhood service,
with Rev. W. F. Dunkle as guest speaker; Boy Scout
Sabbath, March 3; Daughters of Israel Sabbath, March
10; joint service at Center, with Chaplain I. Rackovsky
as guest speaker, March 17; Hadassah Sabbath, March
31; Hebrew School Graduation Sabbath, April 28; two
Young Judea Sabbaths; Mother’s Day Sabbath, May 12.
During Passover the two sedorim in the Center audi-
torium were attended by 600 servicemen. Chaplain J. K.
Shankman, Rabbi I. L. Kaplan and Rabbi Jacob Kaplan
(of Miami) assisted Rabbi Margolis in conducting the
ritual . . . Yiskor services were well attended . . . For
Shovuoth, however, attendance was rather small, except
for Yiskor . . . The Sunday night Confirmation service
was beautiful and impressive ... A D-Day service took
place the evening of June 6, when a large number of
congregants participated in special prayers of victory.
Inter-Faith Activities
For the past seven years Rabbi Margolis has been con-
ducting the Jewish Center radio program over Station
WJAX. It has met with a warm response from Chris-
tian friends who state that they listen to it regularly.
Many of the talks during the past year dealt with the
Zionist issue and were calculated to inform non-Jews
correctly on the question.
Rev. William Dunkle, Jr., a prominent Christian min-
ister, spoke at the Brotherhood Week service held in the
Center—the only event of its kind in the city. Later Rev.
Dunkle invited the Rabbi to explain the customs and
meaning of Passover at vesper services, on Palm Sunday
in the Snyder Memorial Church . . . Rabbi Margolis gave
the closing prayer and benediction at the American Le-
gion memorial services in Hemming Park, Decoration
Day, May 30 . . . He participated in preliminary steps
looking toward the formation of a Jacksonville Round
Table of the National Conference of Christians and
Jews.
===== PAGE 164 =====
Temple Ahavath Chesed
Officers and Trustees
Joel Richards Julien Benjamin
Types of Judaism
As in all other phases of life, American Jewry evidences a wide diver-
gence of opinion in regard to religious tenets and practices. There are cer-
tain fundamental beliefs common to all Jewish groups, such as the belief in
one God, in the brotherhood of mankind, in the moral efficacy of the Ten
Commandments and in the continuity of Jewish religious life throughout the
ages. However, as regards customs and ceremonials, and the relative import-
anee of such matters as tradition and nationhood, the Jews may be divided
into the following three groups.
ORTHODOXY represents those Jewish people who seek faithfully to
observe the beliefs, practices and customs decreed by Moses in the Torah and
interpreted by recognized leaders, scholars and rabbis from generation to
generation. This group is by far the most numerous among the Jews in
America . . . REFORM JUDAISM, while acknowledging the same sources
and foundations as Orthodoxy, stresses the principles of change, growth and
evolution in Jewish as in all religious thought. It strives to give a new in-
terpretation to ritual and ceremonial observances in line with Judaism s funda-
mental principles of social justice, peace and human brotherhood. Midway
between Orthodoxy and Reform is CONSERVATIVE JEWRY. This rep-
resents the group that does not wish to adhere to the strict interpretations
and exacting standards of Orthodoxy nor follow Reform in its non-adherence
to traditional practices.
Max Meyer
Albert Edwards
